<tr>
<th scope="row">Rank</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:US-O4_insignia.svg" class="image"><img alt="US-O4 insignia.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/US-O4_insignia.svg/18px-US-O4_insignia.svg.png" width="18" height="19" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/US-O4_insignia.svg/27px-US-O4_insignia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/US-O4_insignia.svg/36px-US-O4_insignia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="524" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Major_(United_States)" title="Major (United States)">Major</a> <small>(Army)</small><br />
<a href="/wiki/File:US-O6_insignia.svg" class="image"><img alt="US-O6 insignia.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/US-O6_insignia.svg/18px-US-O6_insignia.svg.png" width="18" height="9" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/US-O6_insignia.svg/27px-US-O6_insignia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/US-O6_insignia.svg/36px-US-O6_insignia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="951" data-file-height="484" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">Colonel</a> <small>(Militia)</small></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Battles/wars</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a><br />
&#160;• <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Battle of Trenton</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>James Monroe</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/oʊ/ long 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; April 28, 1758 – July 4, 1831) was an American statesman who served from 1817 to 1825 as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">fifth President of the United States</a>. Monroe was the last president among the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_dynasty" title="Virginia dynasty">Virginian dynasty</a>; he also represented the end of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_date_of_birth#Generation" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the United States by date of birth">Republican Generation</a> in that office.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Born in <a href="/wiki/Westmoreland_County,_Virginia" title="Westmoreland County, Virginia">Westmoreland County, Virginia</a>, Monroe was of the planter class and fought in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. He was wounded in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Battle of Trenton</a> with a musket ball to his shoulder. After studying law under <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> from 1780 to 1783, he served as a <a href="/wiki/List_of_delegates_to_the_Continental_Congress" title="List of delegates to the Continental Congress">delegate</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a>.</p>
<p>As an <a href="/wiki/Anti-Federalism" title="Anti-Federalism">anti-federalist</a> delegate to the Virginia convention that considered ratification of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>, Monroe opposed <a href="/wiki/Ratification" title="Ratification">ratification</a>, claiming it gave too much power to the central government. He took an active part in the new government, and in 1790 he was elected to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> of <a href="/wiki/1st_United_States_Congress" title="1st United States Congress">the first United States Congress</a>, where he joined the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republicans</a>. He gained experience as an executive as the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Virginia" title="Governor of Virginia">Governor of Virginia</a> and rose to national prominence as a diplomat in France, when he helped negotiate the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> in 1803. During the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, Monroe served in critical roles as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> under President <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hart1_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hart1-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Facing little opposition from the fractured <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>, Monroe was <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1816" title="United States presidential election, 1816">easily elected</a> president in 1816, winning over 80 percent of the electoral vote and becoming the last president during the <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a> era of American politics. As president, he sought to ease partisan tensions, embarking on a tour of the country that was well received. With the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">Treaty of 1818</a>, under the successful diplomacy of his Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, the United States extended its reach from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, by acquiring harbor and fishing rights in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a>; the United States and Britain jointly occupied the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Country" title="Oregon Country">Oregon Country</a>. In addition to the acquisition of Florida, the 1819 <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a> secured the border of the United States along the <a href="/wiki/42nd_parallel_north" title="42nd parallel north">42nd Parallel</a> to the Pacific Ocean and represented America's first determined attempt at creating an "American global empire".<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_p1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks_p1-3">[3]</a></sup> As nationalism surged, partisan fury subsided, and the "<a href="/wiki/Era_of_Good_Feelings" title="Era of Good Feelings">Era of Good Feelings</a>" ensued, until the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1819" title="Panic of 1819">Panic of 1819</a> struck, and a <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">dispute over the admission of Missouri</a> embroiled the country in 1820. Nonetheless, Monroe <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1820" title="United States presidential election, 1820">won near-unanimous reelection</a>.</p>
<p>Monroe supported the founding of <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">colonies in Africa for freed slaves</a> that would eventually form the nation of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, whose capital, <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a>, is named in his honor. In 1823, he announced the United States' opposition to any European intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">recently independent countries</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>, which became a landmark in American foreign policy. His presidency concluded the first period of American presidential history before the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian democracy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Party_System" title="Second Party System">Second Party System</a> era. Following his retirement in 1825, Monroe was plagued by financial difficulties. He died in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> on July 4, 1831. He has been <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of presidents of the United States">ranked</a> in the aggregate by scholars as the 16th most successful president.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg/170px-JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg/255px-JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg/340px-JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marker designating the site of James Monroe's birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Hall,_Virginia" title="Monroe Hall, Virginia">Monroe Hall, Virginia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>James Monroe was born on April 28, 1758, in his parents' house located in a wooded area of Westmoreland County, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. The marked site is one mile from the unincorporated community known today as <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Hall,_Virginia" title="Monroe Hall, Virginia">Monroe Hall, Virginia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe_Family_Home_Site" title="James Monroe Family Home Site">James Monroe Family Home Site</a> was listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> in 1979. His father Spence Monroe (1727–1774) was a moderately prosperous planter who also practiced carpentry. His mother Elizabeth Jones (1730–1774) married Spence Monroe in 1752 and they had five children: Elizabeth, James, Spence, Andrew, and Joseph Jones.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harry_Ammon_1971_p._577_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harry_Ammon_1971_p._577-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>His paternal great-grandfather Patrick Andrew Monroe emigrated to America from <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> in the mid-17th century. In 1650 he patented a large tract of land in Washington Parish, <a href="/wiki/Westmoreland_County,_Virginia" title="Westmoreland County, Virginia">Westmoreland County, Virginia</a>. Monroe's mother was the daughter of a wealthy <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Welsh</a> immigrant who had settled in nearby <a href="/wiki/King_George_County,_Virginia" title="King George County, Virginia">King George County, Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Also among James Monroe's ancestors were <a href="/wiki/French_Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="French Huguenot">French Huguenot</a> immigrants, who came to Virginia in 1700.<sup id="cite_ref-Harry_Ammon_1971_p._577_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harry_Ammon_1971_p._577-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>At age eleven, Monroe was enrolled in the lone school in the county. Monroe attended this school for only eleven weeks a year, as his labor was needed on the farm. During this time, Monroe formed a lifelong friendship with an older classmate, <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>. Monroe's mother died in 1772, and his father died two years later. Though he inherited property from both of his parents, the sixteen-year-old Monroe was forced to withdraw from school to support his younger brothers. His childless maternal uncle, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Jones_(Virginia)" title="Joseph Jones (Virginia)">Joseph Jones</a>, became a surrogate father to Monroe and his siblings. A member of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Burgesses" class="mw-redirect" title="Virginia House of Burgesses">Virginia House of Burgesses</a>, Jones took Monroe to the capital of <a href="/wiki/Williamsburg,_Virginia" title="Williamsburg, Virginia">Williamsburg, Virginia</a> and enrolled him in the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="College of William and Mary">College of William and Mary</a>. Jones also introduced Monroe to important Virginians such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Henry" title="Patrick Henry">Patrick Henry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>. In 1774, opposition to the British government grew in the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> in reaction to the "<a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">Intolerable Acts</a>," and Virginia sent a delegation to the <a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">First Continental Congress</a>. Monroe became involved in the opposition to <a href="/wiki/John_Murray,_4th_Earl_of_Dunmore" title="John Murray, 4th Earl of Dunmore">Lord Dunmore</a>, the colonial governor of Virginia, and he took part in the storming of the <a href="/wiki/Governor%27s_Palace_(Williamsburg,_Virginia)" title="Governor's Palace (Williamsburg, Virginia)">Governor's Palace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolutionary_War_service">Revolutionary War service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Revolutionary War service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In early 1776, about a year and a half after his enrollment, Monroe dropped out of college and joined the 3rd Virginia Regiment in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> As the fledgling army valued literacy in its officers, Monroe was commissioned with the rank of lieutenant, serving under Captain <a href="/wiki/William_Washington" title="William Washington">William Washington</a>. After months of training, Monroe and seven hundred Virginia infantrymen were called north to serve in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_and_New_Jersey_campaign" title="New York and New Jersey campaign">New York and New Jersey campaign</a>. Shortly after the Virginians arrived, Washington led the army in a retreat from <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> into New Jersey and then across the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a> into Pennsylvania. In December, Monroe took part in a <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_crossing_of_the_Delaware_River" title="George Washington's crossing of the Delaware River">surprise attack</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Hessian_(soldier)" title="Hessian (soldier)">Hessian encampment</a>. Though the attack was successful, Monroe suffered a severed artery in the battle and nearly died. In the aftermath of the battle, Washington cited Monroe and Washington for their bravery, and promoted Monroe to the rank of captain. After his wounds healed, Monroe returned to Virginia to recruit his own company of soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Monroe's participation in the battle was memorialized in <a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>'s painting, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton,_December_26,_1776" title="The Capture of the Hessians at Trenton, December 26, 1776">The Capture of the Hessians at Trenton, December 26, 1776</a></i>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Leutze" title="Emanuel Leutze">Emanuel Leutze</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware" title="Washington Crossing the Delaware">Washington Crossing the Delaware</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg/350px-The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg" width="350" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg/525px-The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg/700px-The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1992" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton_December_26_1776.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a> painted <i><a href="/wiki/The_Capture_of_the_Hessians_at_Trenton,_December_26,_1776" title="The Capture of the Hessians at Trenton, December 26, 1776">The Capture of the Hessians at Trenton, December 26, 1776</a></i> showing Captain <a href="/wiki/William_Washington" title="William Washington">William Washington</a>, with wounded hand, on the right and Lt. Monroe, severely wounded and helped by Dr. Riker, left of center</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lacking the wealth to induce soldiers to join his company, Monroe instead asked his uncle to return him to the front. Monroe was assigned to the staff of General <a href="/wiki/William_Alexander,_Lord_Stirling" title="William Alexander, Lord Stirling">William Alexander, Lord Stirling</a>. During this time, Monroe formed a close friendship with the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a>, a French volunteer who encouraged Monroe to view the war as part of a wider struggle against religious and political tyranny. Monroe served in the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_campaign" title="Philadelphia campaign">Philadelphia campaign</a> and spent the winter of 1777-1778 at the encampment of <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge" title="Valley Forge">Valley Forge</a>, sharing a log hut with Marshall. After serving in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monmouth" title="Battle of Monmouth">Battle of Monmouth</a>, the destitute Monroe resigned his commission in December 1778 and joined his uncle in Philadelphia. After the British <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Savannah" title="Capture of Savannah">captured Savannah</a>, the Virginia legislature decided to raise four regiments, and Monroe returned to his native state, hoping to receive his own command. With letters of recommendation from Washington, Stirling, and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, Monroe received a commission as a lieutenant colonel and was expected to lead one of the regiments, but recruitment again proved to be an issue. On the advice of Jones, Monroe returned to Williamsburg to study law, becoming a protege of Virginia Governor Thomas Jefferson.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the British increasingly focusing their operations in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern colonies</a>, the Virginians moved the capital to the more defensible city of <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>, and Monroe accompanied Jefferson to the new capital. As Governor of Virginia, Jefferson held command over the state's militia, and he appointed Monroe to the rank of colonel, and Monroe established a messenger network to coordinate with the Continental Army and other state militias. Still unable to raise an army due to a lack of interested recruits, Monroe traveled to his home in King George County, and thus was not present for the British <a href="/wiki/Raid_of_Richmond" title="Raid of Richmond">raid of Richmond</a>. As both the Continental Army and the Virginia militia had an abundance of officers, Monroe did not serve during the <a href="/wiki/Yorktown_campaign" title="Yorktown campaign">Yorktown campaign</a>, and, much to his frustration, Monroe did not take part in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">Siege of Yorktown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe resumed studying law under <a href="/wiki/Jefferson" class="mw-disambig" title="Jefferson">Jefferson</a>, and continued until 1783.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-log-cabin-76_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-log-cabin-76-14">[14]</a></sup> He was not particularly interested in legal theory or practice, but chose to take it up because he thought it offered "the most immediate rewards" and could ease his path to wealth, social standing, and political influence.<sup id="cite_ref-log-cabin-76_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-log-cabin-76-14">[14]</a></sup> After passing the bar, he practiced law in <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">Fredericksburg, Virginia</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Marriage and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On February 16, 1786 Monroe married <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Kortright_Monroe" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth Kortright Monroe">Elizabeth Kortright</a> (1768–1830) in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> She was the daughter of Hannah Aspinwall Kortright and Laurence Kortright, a wealthy trader and former British officer. He met her while serving in the Continental Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a brief honeymoon on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Long Island, New York">Long Island, New York</a>, the Monroes returned to New York City to live with her father until Congress adjourned. They then moved to Virginia, settling in <a href="/wiki/Charlottesville,_Virginia" title="Charlottesville, Virginia">Charlottesville, Virginia</a> in 1789. The Monroe estate in Charlottesville is now known as <a href="/wiki/Brown_College_at_Monroe_Hill#Activities_and_history" title="Brown College at Monroe Hill">Monroe Hill</a>. The Monroes had the following children:<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Eliza Kortright Monroe Hay (1786–1840): Eliza was born in Fredericksburg, Virginia, in 1786, and was educated in Paris at the school of <a href="/wiki/Madame_Campan" class="mw-redirect" title="Madame Campan">Madame Campan</a> during the time her father was the United States Ambassador to France. In 1808 she married <a href="/wiki/George_Hay_(Virginia)" title="George Hay (Virginia)">George Hay</a>, a prominent Virginia attorney who had served as prosecutor in the trial of <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> and later U.S. District Judge.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li>James Spence Monroe (1799–1800): a son who died 16 months after birth.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li>Maria Hester Monroe (1804–1850): married her cousin <a href="/wiki/Samuel_L._Gouverneur" title="Samuel L. Gouverneur">Samuel L. Gouverneur</a> on March 8, 1820, in the first wedding of a president's child in the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plantations_and_slavery">Plantations and slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Plantations and slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OakHillfront.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/OakHillfront.jpg/220px-OakHillfront.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/OakHillfront.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="168" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OakHillfront.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oak Hill Mansion</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Monroe sold his small inherited Virginia plantation in 1783 to enter law and politics. He later fulfilled his youthful dream of becoming the owner of a large plantation and wielding great political power, but his plantation was never profitable. Although he owned much more land and many more slaves, and speculated in property, he was rarely on-site to oversee the operations. Overseers treated the slaves harshly to force production, but the plantations barely broke even. Monroe incurred debts by his lavish and expensive lifestyle and often sold property (including slaves) to pay them off.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Overseers moved or separated slave families from different Monroe plantations in accordance with production and maintenance needs of each satellite plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p159.E2.80.93160_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p159.E2.80.93160-23">[23]</a></sup> One of Monroe's slaves named Daniel often ran away from his plantation in <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albermarle County</a>, to visit other slaves or separated family members.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p159.E2.80.93160_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p159.E2.80.93160-23">[23]</a></sup> Monroe commonly referred to Daniel as a "scoundrel" and described the "worthlessness" of Daniel as a runaway slave.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p159.E2.80.93160_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p159.E2.80.93160-23">[23]</a></sup> The practice of moving and separating slave families was common treatment of slaves in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p159.E2.80.93160_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p159.E2.80.93160-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early political career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virginia_politics">Virginia politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Virginia politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Monroe was elected to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Delegates" title="Virginia House of Delegates">Virginia House of Delegates</a> in 1782. After serving on Virginia's Executive Council,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200512-13_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200512-13-24">[24]</a></sup> he was elected to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a> in November 1783 and served in Annapolis until Congress convened Trenton, New Jersey in June 1784. He had served a total of three years when he finally retired from that office by the rule of rotation.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> By that time, the government was meeting in the temporary capital of <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. While serving in Congress, Monroe became an advocate for western expansion, and played a key role in the writing and passage of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a>. The ordinance created the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">Northwest Territory</a>, providing for federal administration of the territories West of Pennsylvania and North of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. During this period, Jefferson continued to serve as a mentor to Monroe, and, at Jefferson's prompting, he befriended another prominent Virginian, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200513-16_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200513-16-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe resigned from Congress in 1786 to focus on his legal career, and he became an attorney for the state. In 1787, Monroe won election to another term in the Virginia House of Delegates. Though he had become outspoken in his desire to reform the Articles, he was unable to attend the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Philadelphia Convention</a> due to his work obligations.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1788, Monroe became a delegate to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Ratifying_Convention" title="Virginia Ratifying Convention">Virginia Ratifying Convention</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200516-17_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200516-17-28">[28]</a></sup> In Virginia, the struggle over the ratification of the proposed Constitution involved more than a simple clash between federalists and <a href="/wiki/Anti-federalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-federalists">anti-federalists</a>. Virginians held a full spectrum of opinions about the merits of the proposed change in national government. Washington and Madison were leading supporters; <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Henry" title="Patrick Henry">Patrick Henry</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Mason" title="George Mason">George Mason</a> were leading opponents. Those who held the middle ground in the ideological struggle became the central figures. Led by Monroe and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Pendleton" title="Edmund Pendleton">Edmund Pendleton</a>, these "federalists who are for amendments," criticized the absence of a <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_rights" title="Bill of rights">bill of rights</a> and worried about surrendering taxation powers to the central government.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> After Madison reversed himself and promised to pass a bill of rights, the Virginia convention ratified the constitution by a narrow vote, though Monroe himself voted against it. Virginia was tenth state to ratify the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a>, and all thirteen states eventually ratified the document.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Henry and other anti-federalists hoped to elect a Congress that would amend the Constitution to take away most of the powers it had been granted ("commit suicide on [its] own authority," as Madison put it). Henry recruited Monroe to run against Madison for a House seat in the <a href="/wiki/1st_United_States_Congress" title="1st United States Congress">First Congress</a>, and he had the Virginia legislature <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering_in_the_United_States" title="Gerrymandering in the United States">draw</a> a <a href="/wiki/Congressional_district" title="Congressional district">congressional district</a> designed to elect Monroe. During the campaign, Madison and Monroe often traveled together, and the election did not destroy their friendship. Madison prevailed over Monroe, taking 1,308 votes compared to Monroe's 972 votes. Following his defeat, Monroe returned to his legal duties and developed his farm in Charlottesville. After the death of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Grayson" title="William Grayson">William Grayson</a> in 1790, Monroe was elected to serve the remainder of Grayson's term.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_Washington" title="Presidency of George Washington">presidency of George Washington</a>, U.S. politics became increasingly polarized between the supporters of Secretary of State Jefferson and the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalists</a>, led by Secretary of the Treasury Alexander Hamilton. Monroe stood firmly with Jefferson in opposing Hamilton's strong central government and strong executive. As the 1790s progressed, the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a> came to dominate U.S. foreign policy, as the naval battles between Britain and France threatened U.S. trade with Europe. Like most other Jeffersonians, Monroe supported the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, but Hamilton's followers tended to sympathize more with Britain. In 1794, hoping to find a way to avoid war with both countries, Washington appointed Monroe as his <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_France" title="United States Ambassador to France">ambassador to France</a>. At the same time, he appointed the anglophile Federalist <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a> as his <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom">Ambassador to the United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ambassador_to_France">Ambassador to France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ambassador to France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_(1758-1831).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg/220px-James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg" width="220" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg/330px-James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg/440px-James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1076" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_(1758-1831).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The earliest preserved portrait of James Monroe as Minister Plenipotentiary to France in 1794.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After arriving in France, Monroe addressed the <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a>, receiving a standing applause for his speech celebrating <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>. He experienced several early diplomatic successes, including the protection of U.S. trade from French attacks. He also used his influence to win the release of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adrienne_de_La_Fayette" title="Adrienne de La Fayette">Adrienne de La Fayette</a>, the wife of the Marquis de Lafayette.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Months after Monroe arrived in France, the U.S. and Great Britain concluded the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a>, outraging both the French and Monroe—not fully informed about the treaty prior to its publication. Despite the undesirable effects of the Jay Treaty on Franco-American relations, Monroe won French support for U.S. navigational rights on the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>—the mouth of which was controlled by <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>—and in 1795 the U.S. and Spain signed <a href="/wiki/Pinckney%27s_Treaty" title="Pinckney's Treaty">Pinckney's Treaty</a>. The treaty granted the U.S. limited rights to use the port of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200529-34_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200529-34-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frustrated by Monroe's inability to convince the French of the benign nature of the Jay Treaty, Washington recalled Monroe in November 1796. He returned to the United States, where he wrote a 400-page defense of his tenure as ambassador, criticizing Washington's desire to pursue closer relations with Britain at the expense of relations with France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200534-38_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200534-38-35">[35]</a></sup> Monroe became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a>, a political party organized by Jefferson in opposition to the Federalists.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Returning to his home in Charlottesville, he resumed his dual careers as a farmer and lawyer.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_Virginia_and_diplomat">Governor of Virginia and diplomat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Governor of Virginia and diplomat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Monroe was elected <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Virginia" title="Governor of Virginia">Governor of Virginia</a> in 1799, serving his first term from 1799 to 1802—he was reelected in 1811.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> He called out the state militia to suppress <a href="/wiki/Gabriel%27s_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Gabriel's Rebellion">Gabriel's Rebellion</a>. Gabriel and 26 other enslaved people who participated were all hanged for treason. Monroe thought that foreign and Federalist elements had created the <a href="/wiki/Quasi_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Quasi War">Quasi War</a> of 1798–1800 and were behind efforts to prevent the election of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> as president in 1800. As governor he considered using the Virginia militia to force the outcome in favor of Jefferson.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Federalists were likewise suspicious of Monroe, some viewing him at best as a French dupe and at worst a traitor.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after the end of Monroe's gubernatorial tenure, President Jefferson sent Monroe back to France to assist Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Robert_Livingston_(1746%E2%80%931813)" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Livingston (1746–1813)">Robert R. Livingston</a> in negotiating the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a>. While Livingston originally sought to acquire only New Orleans (which France had acquired in the 1800 <a href="/wiki/Third_Treaty_of_San_Ildefonso" title="Third Treaty of San Ildefonso">Treaty of San Ildefonso</a>), Monroe and Livingston accepted <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>'s offer to buy the entire <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Territory" title="Louisiana Territory">Louisiana Territory</a>. Monroe also tried to buy <a href="/wiki/East_Florida" title="East Florida">East Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Florida" title="West Florida">West Florida</a> from Spain, but numerous U.S.-Spanish territorial disputes and a lack of support from France made the Spanish unwilling to sell the territories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200540-44_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200540-44-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe served as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Great_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Ambassador to Great Britain">Minister to the Court of St. James's</a> from 1803 to 1807. In 1806 he negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Monroe%E2%80%93Pinkney_Treaty" title="Monroe–Pinkney Treaty">Monroe–Pinkney Treaty</a> with Great Britain. It would have extended the Jay Treaty of 1794 which had expired after ten years. Jefferson had fought the Jay Treaty intensely in 1794–95 because he felt it would allow the British to subvert <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">American republicanism</a>. The treaty had produced ten years of peace and highly lucrative trade for American merchants, but Jefferson was still opposed. When Monroe and the British signed the new treaty in December 1806, Jefferson refused to submit it to the Senate for ratification. Although the treaty called for ten more years of trade between the United States and the British Empire and gave American merchants guarantees that would have been good for business, Jefferson was unhappy that it did not end the hated British practice of <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">impressment</a> of American sailors, and refused to give up the potential weapon of commercial warfare against Britain. The president made no attempt to obtain another treaty, and as a result, the two nations drifted from peace toward the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> After the failure of the treaty, Monroe returned to the United States in 1807. Monroe was severely pained by the administration's repudiation of the treaty, and Monroe fell out with Secretary of State James Madison.<sup id="cite_ref-leibiger_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leibiger-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1808_election_and_the_Quids">1808 election and the Quids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1808 election and the Quids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Democratic-Republican Party was increasingly factionalized, with "<a href="/wiki/Old_Republicans" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Republicans">Old Republicans</a>" or "Quids" denouncing the Jefferson administration for abandoning true republican principles. The Quids, seeing that Monroe's foreign policy had been rejected by Jefferson, tried to enlist Monroe in their cause. The plan was to run Monroe for president in the 1808 election in cooperation with the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>, which had a strong base in New England. <a href="/wiki/John_Randolph_of_Roanoke" title="John Randolph of Roanoke">John Randolph of Roanoke</a> led the Quid effort to stop Jefferson's choice of <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>. However, the regular Democratic-Republicans overcame the Quids in the nominating caucus, kept control of the party in Virginia, and protected Madison's base. Monroe was not a candidate for president, and Madison was elected.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> After the election Monroe quickly reconciled with Jefferson, but did not speak with Madison, a former friend, until 1810.<sup id="cite_ref-leibiger_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leibiger-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_State_and_Secretary_of_War">Secretary of State and Secretary of War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Secretary of State and Secretary of War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Monroe returned to the Virginia House of Burgesses and was elected to another term as governor in 1811, but served only four months. In April 1811, Madison appointed Monroe as Secretary of State in hopes of shoring up the support of the more radical factions of the Democratic-Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-leibiger_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leibiger-43">[43]</a></sup> Though Monroe had sought long-term peace between the U.S. and Britain during much of Jefferson's presidency, as Secretary of State he increasingly pressed for war against Britain, which had continued the impressment of U.S. sailors. The <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> began after the U.S. <a href="/wiki/United_States_declaration_of_war_upon_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States declaration of war upon the United Kingdom">declared war</a> on Britain in June 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200549-51_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200549-51-45">[45]</a></sup> The war went very badly, and when the British burned the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">U.S. Capitol</a> and the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> on August 24, 1814, Madison removed John Armstrong as Secretary of War and turned to Monroe for help, appointing him <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> on September 27.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200552-53_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200552-53-46">[46]</a></sup> Monroe resigned as Secretary of State on October 1, 1814, but no successor was ever appointed and thus from October 1, 1814 to February 28, 1815, Monroe effectively held both Cabinet posts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200553-54_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200553-54-47">[47]</a></sup> Monroe formulated plans for an offensive invasion of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> to win the war, but a peace treaty was ratified in February 1815, before any armies moved north. Monroe therefore resigned as Secretary of War on March 15, 1815 and was formally reappointed Secretary of State. Having served in two key cabinet posts, Monroe established himself as Madison's likely successor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200553-55_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200553-55-48">[48]</a></sup> He remained at the State Department until March 4, 1817, when he began his term as the new President of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200556-57_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200556-57-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_elections">Presidential elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Presidential elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1816">Election of 1816</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Election of 1816">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1816" title="United States presidential election, 1816">United States presidential election, 1816</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Congress of the United States">Congressional</a> nominating caucus experienced little opposition during the administrations of Jefferson and Madison, but this situation changed in the election year of 1816. An indeterminate number of anti-Virginia Republicans, led by the New York delegation, objected to the caucus system along with the Federalists. Disorganization and failure to agree on <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Tompkins" class="mw-redirect" title="Daniel Tompkins">Daniel Tompkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> or another possible contender weakened opposition to Monroe. The boycott by Virginia delegates of the March 12 caucus removed the chances of Monroe's opponents, and he received the caucus nomination four days later.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> in disarray due to the unpopularity of their opposition to the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, Monroe easily won the election.<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-51">[51]</a></sup> The Federalists did not even name a candidate, though <a href="/wiki/Rufus_King" title="Rufus King">Rufus King</a> of New York did run in opposition to Monroe under the Federalist banner.<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-51">[51]</a></sup> As a result, King won only three states: Connecticut, Delaware, and Massachusetts. The total <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a> vote came in at 183 for Monroe and 34 for King.<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1820">Election of 1820</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Election of 1820">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1820" title="United States presidential election, 1820">United States presidential election, 1820</a></div>
<p>The collapse of the Federalists left Monroe with no organized opposition at the end of his first term, and he ran for reelection unopposed,<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_51-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-51">[51]</a></sup> the only president other than <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">Washington</a> to do so. A single elector from New Hampshire, <a href="/wiki/William_Plumer" title="William Plumer">William Plumer</a>, cast a vote for <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, preventing a unanimous vote in the Electoral College.<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_51-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-51">[51]</a></sup> He did so because he thought Monroe was incompetent. Later in the century, the story arose that he had cast his dissenting vote so that only George Washington would have the honor of unanimous election. Plumer never mentioned Washington in his speech explaining his vote to the other New Hampshire electors.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency">Presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_James_Monroe" title="Presidency of James Monroe">Presidency of James Monroe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -47px; left: -35px; width: 300px"><a href="/wiki/File:MONROE,_James-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Monroe as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/300px-MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="300" height="367" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/450px-MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/600px-MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7912" data-file-height="9681" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MONROE,_James-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:MONROE, James-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:MONROE, James-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Monroe as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_affairs">Domestic affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Domestic affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic-Republican_Party_dominance">Democratic-Republican Party dominance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Democratic-Republican Party dominance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Monroe largely ignored old party lines in making federal appointments, which reduced political tensions and augmented the sense of "oneness" that pervaded the nation. He made two long national tours to build national trust. At Boston, his 1817 visit was hailed as the beginning of an "<a href="/wiki/Era_of_Good_Feelings" title="Era of Good Feelings">Era of Good Feelings</a>." Frequent stops on these tours included ceremonies of welcome and expressions of good will. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Federalist_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> continued to fade during his administration; it maintained its vitality and organizational integrity in Delaware and a few localities, but lacked influence in national politics. Lacking serious opposition, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a>'s Congressional caucus stopped meeting, and for practical purposes the Party stopped operating.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Missouri_Compromise">Missouri Compromise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Missouri Compromise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a></div>
<p>In February 1819, a bill to enable the people of the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Territory" title="Missouri Territory">Missouri Territory</a> to draft a constitution and form a government preliminary to admission into the Union came before the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>. During these proceedings, Congressman <a href="/wiki/James_Tallmadge,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="James Tallmadge, Jr.">James Tallmadge, Jr.</a> of New York "tossed a bombshell into the Era of Good Feelings"<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> by offering amendments (known collectively as the <a href="/wiki/Tallmadge_Amendment" title="Tallmadge Amendment">Tallmadge Amendment</a>) prohibiting the further introduction of slaves into Missouri, and requiring that all future children of slave parents therein should be free at the age of twenty-five years. After three days of rancorous and sometimes bitter debate, the bill, with Tallmadge’s amendments, passed. The measure then went to the Senate, where both amendments were rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> A House -Senate <a href="/wiki/Conference_committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Conference committee">conference committee</a> was unable to resolve the disagreements on the bill, and so the entire measure failed.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The amendment instantly exposed the polarization among <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_Republicans" class="mw-redirect" title="Jeffersonian Republicans">Jeffersonian Republicans</a> over the future of slavery in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[a]</a></sup> Northern Jeffersonian Republicans formed a coalition across factional lines with remnants of the Federalists. Southern Jeffersonians united in almost unanimous opposition. The ensuing debates pitted the northern "restrictionists" (antislavery legislators who wished to bar slavery from the Louisiana territories) and southern "anti-restrictionists" (proslavery legislators who rejected any interference by Congress inhibiting slavery expansion).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the following session (1819–1820), the House passed a similar bill with an amendment, introduced on January 26, 1820, by <a href="/wiki/John_W._Taylor_(politician)" title="John W. Taylor (politician)">John W. Taylor</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, allowing Missouri into the union as a slave state. The question had been complicated by the admission in December of <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Slave_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave state">slave state</a>, making the number of slave and free states equal. In addition, there was a bill in passage through the House (January 3, 1820) to admit <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Free_state_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free state (United States)">free state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> The Senate decided to connect the two measures. It passed a bill for the admission of Maine with an amendment enabling the people of Missouri to form a state constitution. Before the bill was returned to the House, a second amendment was adopted on the motion of <a href="/wiki/Jesse_B._Thomas" title="Jesse B. Thomas">Jesse B. Thomas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, excluding slavery from the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Territory" title="Louisiana Territory">Louisiana Territory</a> north of the <a href="/wiki/Parallel_36%C2%B030%E2%80%B2_north" title="Parallel 36°30′ north">parallel 36°30′ north</a> (the southern boundary of Missouri), except within the limits of the proposed state of Missouri. The House then approved the bill as amended by the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two houses were at odds not only on the legality of slavery, but also on the inclusion of Maine and Missouri within the same bill. The committee recommended the enactment of two laws, one for the admission of Maine, the other an <a href="/wiki/Enabling_act" title="Enabling act">enabling act</a> for Missouri. They opposed restrictions on slavery except for the Thomas amendment. Both houses agreed, the measures were passed on March 5, 1820, and were signed by the president on March 6.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The question of the final admission of Missouri came up during the session of 1820–1821. The struggle was revived over a clause in Missouri's new constitution (written in 1820) requiring the exclusion of "free negroes and mulattoes" from the state. Through the influence of <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> "The Great Compromiser", an act of admission was finally passed, upon the condition that the exclusionary clause of the Missouri constitution should "never be construed to authorize the passage of any law" impairing the privileges and immunities of any U.S. citizen. This deliberately ambiguous provision is sometimes known as the Second Missouri Compromise.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_improvements">Internal improvements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Internal improvements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As the United States continued to grow, many Americans advocated a system of internal improvements to help the country develop. Monroe agreed the young nation needed an improved infrastructure, including a transportation network in order to grow and thrive economically. However, he discerned no Constitutional authority to build, maintain, and operate a national transportation system. He therefore urged Congress to introduce a constitutional amendment granting it such power. Congress never acted on his suggestion because many legislators thought they already had the implied authority to enact such measures.<sup id="cite_ref-JM:DA_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JM:DA-64">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1822, a bill to authorize the collection of tolls on the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_Road" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumberland Road">Cumberland Road</a> (which provided for yearly improvements to the road) was vetoed by the President. In an elaborate essay, Monroe set forth his views on the constitutional aspects of a policy of internal improvements. Congress might appropriate money, he admitted, but it could not undertake the construction of national works nor assume jurisdiction over them. For the moment, the drift toward a larger participation of the national government in internal improvements was stayed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Two years later, Congress authorized the President to institute surveys for such roads and canals as he believed to be needed for commerce and military defense. No one pleaded more eloquently for a larger conception of the functions of the national government than <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>. He called the attention of his hearers to provisions made for coast surveys and lighthouses on the Atlantic seaboard and deplored the neglect of the interior of the country. Of the other presidential candidates, Jackson voted in the Senate for the general survey bill; and Adams left no doubt in the public mind that he did not reflect the narrow views of his section on this issue. Crawford felt the constitutional scruples which were everywhere being voiced in the South, and followed the old expedient of advocating a constitutional amendment to sanction national internal improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Panic_of_1819">Panic of 1819</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Panic of 1819">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Two years into his presidency, Monroe faced an economic crisis known as the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1819" title="Panic of 1819">Panic of 1819</a>, the first major depression to hit the country since the 1780s. The panic stemmed from declining imports and exports, and sagging agricultural prices<sup id="cite_ref-JM:DA_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JM:DA-64">[63]</a></sup> as global markets readjusted to peacetime production and commerce in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmmon462_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmmon462-66">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz.2C_2008208.2C_215_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz.2C_2008208.2C_215-67">[66]</a></sup> The severity of the economic downturn in the U.S. was compounded by excessive <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculation</a> in public lands,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDangerfield.2C_196582.2C_84.2C_86_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDangerfield.2C_196582.2C_84.2C_86-69">[68]</a></sup> fueled by the unrestrained issue of paper money from banks and business concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz.2C_2008206_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz.2C_2008206-70">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDangerfield.2C_196587_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDangerfield.2C_196587-71">[70]</a></sup> The resulting high unemployment and an increase in bankruptcies and foreclosures provoked popular resentment against banking and business enterprises.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDangerfield.2C_196582-90_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDangerfield.2C_196582-90-72">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> It also exacerbated tensions within the Democratic-Republican Party and aggravated sectional tensions as northerners pressed for higher tariffs while southerners abandoned their support of nationalistic economic programs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Foreign affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bilateral_Treaties">Bilateral Treaties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Bilateral Treaties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rush-Bagot_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Rush-Bagot Treaty">Rush-Bagot Treaty</a> with Great Britain was signed April 20, 1817; it regulated naval armaments on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Champlain" title="Lake Champlain">Lake Champlain</a>, and demilitarized the border between the U.S. and <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">Treaty of 1818</a>, also with Great Britain, was concluded October 20, 1818, and fixed the present <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Canada–United States border">Canada–United States border</a> from <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> at the <a href="/wiki/49th_parallel_north" title="49th parallel north">49th parallel</a>. The accords also established a joint U.S.–British occupation of <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Country" title="Oregon Country">Oregon Country</a> for the next ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-JMforeign_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JMforeign-75">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Russo-American_Treaty_of_1824" title="Russo-American Treaty of 1824">Russo-American Treaty of 1824</a> with <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>, dated April 17, 1824, set the southern limit of Russian sovereignty on the Pacific coast of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Parallel_54%C2%B040%E2%80%B2_north" title="Parallel 54°40′ north">54°40′ parallel</a>. (the present southern tip of the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Panhandle" class="mw-redirect" title="Alaska Panhandle">Alaska Panhandle</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Florida">Spanish Florida</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Spanish Florida">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

</td>
