Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(April 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars" title="Seminole Wars">Seminole Wars</a></div>
<p>Spain had long rejected American efforts to purchase <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Florida</a>. But by 1818, Spain was facing a troubling colonial situation in which the cession of Florida made sense. Spain had been exhausted by the <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">Peninsular War</a> in Europe and needed to rebuild its credibility and presence in its colonies. Revolutionaries in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> were beginning to demand independence. Spain was unwilling to invest further in Florida, encroached on by American settlers, and it worried about the border between <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a> (a large area including today's <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, Central America, and much of the current US Western States) and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. With minor military presence in Florida, Spain was not able to restrain the <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> warriors who routinely crossed the border and raided American villages and farms, as well as protected southern slave refugees from slave owners and traders of the southern United States.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>To stop the <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> Indians based in <a href="/wiki/East_Florida" title="East Florida">East Florida</a> from raiding Georgia settlements and offering havens for runaway slaves, the U.S. Army led increasingly frequent incursions into Spanish territory. This included the 1817–1818 campaign led by General <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> that became known as the <a href="/wiki/First_Seminole_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First Seminole War">First Seminole War</a>. Jackson and a force of Tennessee Militia, under federal orders, attacked <a href="/wiki/Pensacola,_Florida" title="Pensacola, Florida">Pensacola</a>, and captured forts, such as <a href="/wiki/San_Marcos_de_Apalache_Historic_State_Park" title="San Marcos de Apalache Historic State Park">St. Marks</a>, and the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Negro_Fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Negro Fort">Negro Fort</a>" (an abandoned British fort manned by escaped slaves and Seminoles), in Florida that he thought were assisting the raids into American territory. As a result, the U.S. effectively seized control of Northeastern Florida; albeit for purposes of lawful government and administration (in the state of Georgia); but not for the outright annexation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Secretary of State John Quincy Adams said the US had to take control because Florida (along the border of Georgia &amp; Alabama Territory) had become "a derelict open to the occupancy of every enemy, civilized or savage, of the United States, and serving no other earthly purpose than as a post of annoyance to them."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Spain asked for British intervention, but London declined to assist Spain in the negotiations. Some in Monroe's cabinet demanded Jackson's immediate dismissal for invading Florida, but Adams realized that his success had given the U.S. a favorable diplomatic position. Adams was able to negotiate very favorable terms. In 1819 Spain and the United States signed the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a>, that ceded Florida to the United States in return for the assumption of $5,000,000 (about $92,592,593 in 2014 dollars) in claims and the relinquishment of any claims to Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> The treaty <a href="/wiki/Coming_into_force" title="Coming into force">became effective</a> on February 22, 1821.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monroe_Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Monroe Doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(April 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a></div>
<p>After the Napoleonic wars (which ended in 1815), almost all of Spain's and Portugal's colonies in Latin America revolted and declared independence. Americans welcomed this development as a validation of the spirit of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">Republicanism</a>. Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> suggested delaying formal recognition until Florida was secured. The problem of imperial invasion was intensified by a Russian claim to the Pacific coast down to the fifty-first parallel and simultaneous European pressure to have all of Latin America returned to its colonial status.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Monroe informed Congress in March 1822 that permanent stable governments had been established in the <a href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_the_River_Plate" class="mw-redirect" title="United Provinces of the River Plate">United Provinces of the River Plate</a> (the core of present-day <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>), <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. Adams, under Monroe's supervision, wrote the instructions for the ministers (ambassadors) to these new countries. They declared that the policy of the United States was to uphold republican institutions and to seek treaties of commerce on a most-favored-nation basis. The United States would support inter-American congresses dedicated to the development of economic and political institutions fundamentally differing from those prevailing in Europe. The articulation of an "American system" distinct from that of Europe was a basic tenet of Monroe's policy toward Latin America. Monroe took pride as the United States was the first nation to extend recognition and to set an example to the rest of the world for its support of the "cause of liberty and humanity".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Monroe formally announced in his message to Congress on December 2, 1823, what was later called the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>. He proclaimed that the Americas should be free from future European colonization and free from European interference in sovereign countries' affairs. It further stated the United States' intention to stay neutral in wars amongst European powers and their colonies, but to consider new colonies or interference with independent countries in the Americas as hostile acts toward the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[b]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although it is Monroe's most famous contribution to history, the speech was written by Adams, who designed the doctrine in cooperation with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Ammon.2C_James_Monroe.2C_pp_476.E2.80.9392_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ammon.2C_James_Monroe.2C_pp_476.E2.80.9392-81">[79]</a></sup> Monroe and Adams realized that American recognition would not protect the new countries against military intervention to restore Spain's power. In October 1823, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rush" title="Richard Rush">Richard Rush</a>, the American minister in London, advised that Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/George_Canning" title="George Canning">George Canning</a> was proposing that the U.S. and Britain jointly declare their opposition to European intervention. Britain, with its powerful navy, also opposed re-conquest of <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and suggested that the United States join in proclaiming a "hands off" policy. Galvanized by the British initiative, Monroe consulted with American leaders and then formulated a plan with Adams. Ex-Presidents Jefferson and Madison counseled Monroe to accept the offer, but Adams advised, "It would be more candid ... to avow our principles explicitly to Russia and France, than to come in as a cock-boat in the wake of the British man-of-war." Monroe accepted Adams' advice. Not only must Latin America be left alone, he warned, but also Russia must not encroach southward on the Pacific coast. "the American continents," he stated, "by the free and independent condition which they have assumed and maintain, are henceforth not to be considered as subjects for future colonization by any European Power."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The Monroe Doctrine at the time of its adoption thus pertained more to the Russians in North America than to the former Spanish colonies. The result was a system of American isolationism under the sponsorship of the British navy. The Monroe Doctrine held that the United States considered the Western Hemisphere as no longer a place for European colonization; that any future effort to gain further political control in the hemisphere or to violate the independence of existing states would be treated as an act of hostility; and finally that there existed two different and incompatible political systems in the world. The United States, therefore, promised to refrain from intervention in European affairs and demanded Europe to abstain from interfering with American matters. There were few serious European attempts at intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-Ammon.2C_James_Monroe.2C_pp_476.E2.80.9392_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ammon.2C_James_Monroe.2C_pp_476.E2.80.9392-81">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_Cabinet">Administration and Cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Administration and Cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Monroe made balanced Cabinet choices, naming a southerner, <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, as Secretary of War, and a northerner, <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, as Secretary of State. Both proved outstanding, as Adams was a master diplomat<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> and Calhoun completely reorganized the War Department to overcome the serious deficiencies that had hobbled it during the War of 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> Monroe decided on political grounds not to offer <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> the State Department, and Clay turned down the War Department and remained Speaker of the House, so Monroe lacked an outstanding westerner in his cabinet. Monroe was the only president in the 19th century to complete two full terms with the same Vice President.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_James_Monroe" title="List of federal judges appointed by James Monroe">List of federal judges appointed by James Monroe</a></div>
<p>Monroe appointed one justice to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smith_Thompson" title="Smith Thompson">Smith Thompson</a>. He appointed 21 other federal judges, all to <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_court" title="United States district court">United States district courts</a>, as no vacancies occurred on the one circuit court existing at the time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: States admitted to the Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Five new states were <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_the_Union" title="Admission to the Union">admitted to the Union</a> while Monroe was in office:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>&#160;– December 10, 1817<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>&#160;– December 3, 1818<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>&#160;– December 14, 1819<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>&#160;– March 15, 1820<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[c]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>&#160;– August 10, 1821<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Post-presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG/170px-James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG/255px-James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG/340px-James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Monroe once owned a farm at the location of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlottesville,_Virginia" title="Charlottesville, Virginia">Charlottesville</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When his presidency ended on March 4, 1825, James Monroe resided at <a href="/wiki/Brown_College_at_Monroe_Hill" title="Brown College at Monroe Hill">Monroe Hill</a>, what is now included in the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>. He had operated the family farm from 1788 to 1817, but sold it in the first year of his presidency to the new university. He served on the Board of Visitors under Jefferson and under the second rector <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, both former presidents, almost until his death.</p>
<p>Monroe incurred many unliquidated debts during his years of public life. He sold off his Highland Plantation (now called <a href="/wiki/Ash_Lawn-Highland" class="mw-redirect" title="Ash Lawn-Highland">Ash Lawn-Highland</a>). It is now owned by his <i>alma mater</i>, the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="College of William and Mary">College of William and Mary</a>, which has opened it to the public as a historic site. Throughout his life, he was financially insolvent, and this was exacerbated by his wife's poor health.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe was elected as a delegate to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Constitutional_Convention_of_1829-1830" class="mw-redirect" title="Virginia Constitutional Convention of 1829-1830">Virginia Constitutional Convention of 1829-1830</a>. He was one of four delegates elected from the senatorial district made up of his home district of Loudoun and Fairfax County.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> In October 1829, he was elected by the Convention to serve as the presiding officer, until his failing health required him to withdraw on December 8, after which <a href="/wiki/Philip_Pendleton_Barbour" title="Philip Pendleton Barbour">Philip Pendleton Barbour</a> of Orange County was elected presiding officer.</p>
<p>He and his wife lived in <a href="/wiki/Oak_Hill_(James_Monroe_House)" title="Oak Hill (James Monroe House)">Oak Hill</a>, Virginia, until Elizabeth's death on September 23, 1830. In August 1825, the Monroes had received the <a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Lafayette" class="mw-redirect" title="Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a> and President <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> as guests there.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_Grave.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/James_Monroe_Grave.JPG/240px-James_Monroe_Grave.JPG" width="240" height="393" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/James_Monroe_Grave.JPG/360px-James_Monroe_Grave.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/James_Monroe_Grave.JPG/480px-James_Monroe_Grave.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2205" data-file-height="3610" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_Grave.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Monroe's grave at Hollywood Cemetery. <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a>'s grave is visible in the background.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon Elizabeth's death in 1830, Monroe moved to <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> to live with his daughter Maria Hester Monroe Gouverneur who had married <a href="/wiki/Samuel_L._Gouverneur" title="Samuel L. Gouverneur">Samuel L. Gouverneur</a> in the White House. Monroe's health began to slowly fail by the end of the 1820s and John Quincy Adams visited him there in April 1831.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> Adams found him alert and eager to discuss the situation in Europe, but in ill health. Adams cut the visit short when he thought he was tiring Monroe.</p>
<p>Monroe died there from <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> on July 4, 1831, thus becoming the third president to have died on <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Independence Day</a>. His death came 55 years after the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Declaration_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Declaration of Independence">U.S. Declaration of Independence</a> was proclaimed and 5 years after the death of two other Founding Fathers who became Presidents: <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>. Monroe was originally buried in New York at the Gouverneur family's vault in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Marble_Cemetery" title="New York City Marble Cemetery">New York City Marble Cemetery</a>. Twenty-seven years later, in 1858, the body was re-interred to the President's Circle at the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Cemetery_(Richmond,_Virginia)" title="Hollywood Cemetery (Richmond, Virginia)">Hollywood Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe_Tomb" title="James Monroe Tomb">James Monroe Tomb</a> is a U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_beliefs">Religious beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Religious beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"When it comes to Monroe's thoughts on religion," historian Bliss Isely notes, "less is known than that of any other President." No letters survive in which he discussed his religious beliefs. Nor did his friends, family or associates comment on his beliefs. Letters that do survive, such as ones written after the death of his son, contain no discussion of religion.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe was raised in a family that belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> when it was the state church in Virginia before the Revolution. As an adult, he attended <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Episcopal Church in the United States of America">Episcopal</a> churches. Some historians see "deistic tendencies" in his few references to an impersonal God.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> Unlike Jefferson, Monroe was rarely attacked as an atheist or infidel. In 1832 James Renwick Willson, a <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_Church_of_North_America" title="Reformed Presbyterian Church of North America">Reformed Presbyterian</a> minister in Albany, New York, criticized Monroe for having "lived and died like a second-rate Athenian philosopher."<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Secretary of State, Monroe is said to have dismissed <a href="/wiki/Mordecai_Manuel_Noah" title="Mordecai Manuel Noah">Mordecai Manuel Noah</a> in 1815 from his post as consul to <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a> because he was Jewish.<sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-7425-0181-7_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-7425-0181-7-98">[95]</a></sup> Noah protested and gained letters from Adams, Jefferson, and Madison supporting church-state separation and tolerance for Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe may have believed in an interventionist God when he said, "If we persevere...we can not fail, under the favor of a gracious Providence...My fervent <a href="/wiki/Prayers" class="mw-redirect" title="Prayers">prayers</a> to the Almighty that He will be graciously pleased to continue to us that protection which He has already so conspicuously displayed in our favor."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Monroe owned dozens of <a href="/wiki/Slaves" class="mw-redirect" title="Slaves">slaves</a>. According to William Seale, he took several slaves with him to Washington to serve at the White House from 1817 to 1825. This was typical of other slaveholders, as Congress did not provide for domestic staff of the presidents at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 15, 1799, as some <a href="/wiki/Slave_traders" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave traders">slave traders</a> tried to transport a group of slaves from Southampton to Georgia, the <a href="/wiki/Slave_revolt" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave revolt">slaves revolted</a> and killed the traders.<sup id="cite_ref-aptheker_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aptheker-102">[99]</a></sup> According to Scheer's article on the subject, a nearby slave patrol responded and killed ten slaves on the spot in extrajudicial killings without the benefit of trial. Of the initial group, the patrol took five slaves alive. They were tried in an oyer and terminer court without the benefit of a jury,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> and four were convicted. (The fifth pleaded <a href="/wiki/Benefit_of_clergy" title="Benefit of clergy">benefit of clergy</a> and was flogged and branded). Governor Monroe postponed the slaves' executions to check their identities; he granted a pardon to one, and allowed two to hang. The fourth died in jail from exposure to the cold. Scheer says that Monroe "help[ed] secure a modicum of civil protection for slaves sentenced to death for capital crimes."<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Monroe was Governor of Virginia in 1800, hundreds of slaves from Virginia planned to kidnap him, take <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>, and negotiate for their freedom. Due to a storm on August 30, they were unable to attack. What became known as <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Prosser" title="Gabriel Prosser">Gabriel's slave conspiracy</a> became public knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response, Governor Monroe called out the militia; the slave patrols soon captured some slaves accused of involvement. Sidbury says some trials had a few measures to prevent abuses, such as an appointed attorney, but they were "hardly 'fair'". <a href="/wiki/Slave_codes" title="Slave codes">Slave codes</a> prevented slaves from being treated like whites, and they were given quick trials without a jury.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup> Monroe influenced the Executive Council to pardon and sell some slaves instead of hanging them.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup> Historians say the Virginia courts executed between 26 and 35 slaves. None of the executed slaves had killed any whites because the uprising had been foiled before it began.<sup id="cite_ref-aptheker_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aptheker-102">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>As president of Virginia's constitutional convention in the fall of 1829, Monroe reiterated his belief that slavery was a blight which, even as a British colony, Virginia had attempted to eradicate. "What was the origin of our slave population?" he rhetorically asked. "The evil commenced when we were in our Colonial state, but acts were passed by our Colonial Legislature, prohibiting the importation, of more slaves, into the Colony. These were rejected by the Crown." To the dismay of states' rights proponents, he was willing to accept the federal government's financial assistance to emancipate and transport freed slaves to other countries. At the convention, Monroe made his final public statement on slavery, proposing that Virginia <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">emancipate</a> and deport its bondsmen with "the aid of the Union."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe was part of the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">American Colonization Society</a> formed in 1816, the members of which included <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. They found common ground with some abolitionists in supporting colonization. They helped send several thousand freed slaves to the new colony of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> in Africa from 1820 to 1840. Slave owners like Monroe and Jackson wanted to prevent free blacks from encouraging slaves in the South to rebel. With about $100,000 in Federal grant money, the organization also bought land for the freedmen in what is today Liberia.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup> The capital of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> was named <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a> after President Monroe.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_memberships">Honors and memberships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Honors and memberships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Monroe was elected a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Antiquarian_Society" title="American Antiquarian Society">American Antiquarian Society</a> in April 1818.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_memory">Legacy and memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Legacy and memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Since its 1824 renaming in his honor, the capital city of the West African country of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> has been named <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a>. It is the only non-American capital city named after a U.S. President.</li>
<li>On December 12, 1954, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> released a 5¢ <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Issue" title="Liberty Issue">Liberty Issue</a> <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamp</a> honoring Monroe.</li>
<li>There are academic buildings named after him at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mary_Washington" title="University of Mary Washington">University of Mary Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="College of William and Mary">College of William and Mary</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Mason_University" title="George Mason University">George Mason University</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a>. In addition, a statue of Monroe was dedicated in front of Tucker Hall on the campus of William &amp; Mary in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li>Monroe is the namesake of seventeen <a href="/wiki/Monroe_County_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Monroe County (disambiguation)">Monroe counties</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li>The cities of <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Michigan" title="Monroe, Michigan">Monroe, Michigan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Georgia" title="Monroe, Georgia">Monroe, Georgia</a>, incorporated in 1821, and <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Connecticut" title="Monroe, Connecticut">Monroe, Connecticut</a>, incorporated in 1823, are named for him.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fort_Monroe" title="Fort Monroe">Fort Monroe</a> is named for him.</li>
<li>James Monroe Park located on <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Avenue" title="Pennsylvania Avenue">Pennsylvania Avenue</a>, between 20th &amp; I Streets, NW in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> is dedicated in his honor.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Township,_Middlesex_County,_New_Jersey" title="Monroe Township, Middlesex County, New Jersey">Township of Monroe</a>, in central New Jersey, founded in 1838, bears his name as well.</li>
<li>Monroe was the last U.S. President to wear a <a href="/wiki/Wig" title="Wig">powdered wig tied in a queue</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tricorne" title="Tricorne">tricorne hat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culottes" title="Culottes">knee-breeches</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/1775%E2%80%931795_in_fashion#Men.27s_fashion" class="mw-redirect" title="1775–1795 in fashion">style of the late 18th century</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup> That earned him the nickname "The Last Cocked Hat".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup></li>
<li>Monroe is the last president not photographed.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 35em; -webkit-column-width: 35em; column-width: 35em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"[T]he sectional divisions among the Jeffersonian Republicans…offers historical paradoxes…in which hard-line slaveholding Southern Republicans rejected the egalitarian ideals of the slaveholder [Thomas] Jefferson while the antislavery Northern Republicans upheld them – even as Jefferson himself supported slavery's expansion on purportedly antislavery grounds."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> "The most prominent feature of the voting at this stage was its apparently sectional character."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"We owe it, therefore, to candor and to the amicable relations existing between the United States and those powers to declare that we should consider any attempt on their part to extend their system to any portion of this hemisphere as dangerous to our peace and safety. With the existing colonies or dependencies of any European power we have not interfered and shall not interfere, but with the Governments who have declared their independence and maintained it, and whose independence we have, on great consideration and on just principles, acknowledged, we could not view any interposition for the purpose of oppressing them, or controlling in any other manner their destiny, by any European power in any other light than as the manifestation of an unfriendly disposition toward the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Maine is one of 3 states that were set off from already existing states (Kentucky and West Virginia are the others). The <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_General_Court" title="Massachusetts General Court">Massachusetts General Court</a> passed enabling legislation on June 19, 1819 separating the "<a href="/wiki/District_of_Maine" title="District of Maine">District of Maine</a>" from the rest of the State (an action approved by the voters in Maine on July 19, 1819 by 17,001 to 7,132); then, on February 25, 1820, passed a follow-up measure officially accepting the fact of Maine's imminent statehood.<sup id="cite_ref-GP_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GP-88">[86]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_sources">Primary sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Primary sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Monroe, James. <i>The Political Writings of James Monroe.</i> ed. by James P. Lucier, (2002). 863 pp.</li>
<li><i>Writings of James Monroe,</i> edited by Stanislaus Murray Hamilton, ed., 7 vols. (1898–1903) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=g-IoEnhfOYEC">online edition at Google Books</a></li>

(2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dacapopress.com/book/paperback/the-last-founding-father/9780306819186"><i>The Last Founding Father: James Monroe and a Nation's Call to Greatness</i></a>. Da Capo Press.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJames+Monroe&amp;rft.aufirst=Harlow+G.&amp;rft.aulast=Unger&amp;rft.btitle=The+Last+Founding+Father%3A+James+Monroe+and+a+Nation%27s+Call+to+Greatness&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdacapopress.com%2Fbook%2Fpaperback%2Fthe-last-founding-father%2F9780306819186&amp;rft.pub=Da+Capo+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> a new biography.</li>
<li>White, Leonard D. <i>The Jeffersonians: A Study in Administrative History, 1801–1829</i> (1951), explains the operation and organization of federal administration</li>
<li>Whitaker, Arthur P. <i>The United States and the Independence of Latin America</i> (1941)</li>
<li>White, Leonard D. <i>The Jeffersonians: A Study in Administrative History, 1801–1829</i> (1951), explains the operation and organization of federal administration</li>
<li>Wilmerding, Jr., Lucius, <i>James Monroe: Public Claimant</i> (1960) A study regarding Monroe's attempts to get reimbursement for personal expenses and losses from his years in public service after his Presidency ended.</li>

"Jeffersonian Democracy and the Origins of Political Antislavery in the United States: The Missouri Crisis Revisited". <i>The Journal of the Historical Society</i>. <b>IV</b> (3).</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJames+Monroe&amp;rft.atitle=Jeffersonian+Democracy+and+the+Origins+of+Political+Antislavery+in+the+United+States%3A+The+Missouri+Crisis+Revisited&amp;rft.aufirst=Sean&amp;rft.aulast=Wilentz&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Journal+of+the+Historical+Society&amp;rft.ssn=fall&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=IV" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> <span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33">CS1 maint: Date and year (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Date_and_year" title="Category:CS1 maint: Date and year">link</a>)</span></li>
